Churchill observed that an "Iron Curtain" had been drawn across Europe, separating the east from the west.[597] In September 1947, a meeting of East European communist leaders was held in Szklarska Poręba, Poland, from which was formed Cominform to co-ordinate the Communist Parties across Eastern Europe and also in France and Italy.[598] Stalin did not personally attend the meeting, sending Zhdanov in his place.[546] Various East European communists also visited Stalin in Moscow.[599] There, he offered advice on their ideas; for instance he cautioned against the Yugoslav idea for a Balkan federation incorporating Bulgaria and Albania.[599] Stalin had a particularly strained relationship with Yugoslav leader Josip Broz Tito due to the latter's continued calls for Balkan federation and for Soviet aid for the communist forces in the ongoing Greek Civil War.[600] In March 1948, Stalin launched an anti-Tito campaign, accusing the Yugoslav communists of adventurism and deviating from Marxist–Leninist doctrine.[601] At the second Cominform conference, held in Bucharest in June 1948, East European communist leaders all denounced Tito's government, accusing them of being fascists and agents of Western capitalism.[602] Stalin ordered several assassination attempts on Tito's life and contemplated invading Yugoslavia.[603]

Stalin suggested that a unified, but demilitarised, German state be established, hoping that it would either come under Soviet influence or remain neutral.[604] When the US and UK remained opposed to this, Stalin sought to force their hand by blockading Berlin in June 1948.[605] He gambled that the others would not risk war, but they airlifted supplies into West Berlin until May 1949, when Stalin relented and ended the blockade.[589] In September 1949 the Western powers transformed Western Germany into an independent Federal Republic of Germany; in response the Soviets formed East Germany into the German Democratic Republic in October.[604] In accordance with their earlier agreements, the Western powers expected Poland to become an independent state with free democratic elections.[606] In Poland, the Soviets merged various socialist parties into the Polish United Workers' Party, and vote rigging was used to ensure that it secured office.[601] The 1947 Hungarian elections were also rigged, with the Hungarian Working People's Party taking control.[601] In Czechoslovakia, where the communists did have a level of popular support, they were elected the largest party in 1946.[607] Monarchy was abolished in Bulgaria and Romania.[608] Across Eastern Europe, the Soviet model was enforced, with a termination of political pluralism, agricultural collectivisation, and investment in heavy industry.[602] It was aimed for economic autarky within the Eastern Bloc.[602]

Asia
In October 1949, Mao took power in China.[609] With this accomplished, Marxist governments now controlled a third of the world's land mass.[610] Privately, Stalin revealed that he had underestimated the Chinese Communists and their ability to win the civil war, instead encouraging them to make another peace with the KMT.[611] In December 1949, Mao visited Stalin. Initially Stalin refused to repeal the Sino-Soviet Treaty of 1945, which significantly benefited the Soviet Union over China, although in January 1950 he relented and agreed to sign a new treaty between the two countries.[612] Stalin was concerned that Mao might follow Tito's example by pursuing a course independent of Soviet influence, and made it known that if displeased he would withdraw assistance from China; the Chinese desperately needed said assistance after decades of civil war.[613]


Stalin and Mao Zedong depicted on a Chinese postage stamp from 1950
At the end of the Second World War, the Soviet Union and the United States divided up the Korean Peninsula, formerly a Japanese colonial possession, along the 38th parallel, setting up a communist government in the north and a pro-Western government in the south.[614] North Korean leader Kim Il-sung visited Stalin in March 1949 and again in March 1950; he wanted to invade the south and although Stalin was initially reluctant to provide support, he eventually agreed by May 1950.[615] The North Korean Army launched the Korean War by invading the south in June 1950, making swift gains and capturing Seoul.[616] Both Stalin and Mao believed that a swift victory would ensue.[616] The U.S. went to the UN Security Council—which the Soviets were boycotting over its refusal to recognise Mao's government—and secured military support for the South Koreans. U.S. led forces pushed the North Koreans back.[617] Stalin wanted to avoid direct Soviet conflict with the U.S., convincing the Chinese to aid the North.[618]

The Soviet Union was one of the first nations to extend diplomatic recognition to the newly created state of Israel in 1948. When the Israeli ambassador Golda Meir arrived in the USSR, Stalin was angered by the Jewish crowds who gathered to greet her.[619] He was further angered by Israel's growing alliance with the U.S.[620] After Stalin fell out with Israel, he launched an anti-Jewish campaign within the Soviet Union and the Eastern Bloc.[596] In November 1948, he abolished the JAC,[621] and show trials took place for some of its members.[622] The Soviet press engaged in attacks on Zionism, Jewish culture, and "rootless cosmopolitanism",[623] with growing levels of anti-Semitism being expressed across Soviet society.[624] Stalin's increasing tolerance of anti-Semitism may have stemmed from his increasing Russian nationalism or from the recognition that anti-Semitism had proved a useful mobilising tool for Hitler and that he could do the same;[625] he may have increasingly viewed the Jewish people as a "counter-revolutionary" nation whose members were loyal to the U.S.[626] There were rumours, although they have never been substantiated, that Stalin was planning on deporting all Soviet Jews to the Jewish Autonomous Region in Birobidzhan, eastern Siberia.[627]

Final years: 1950–1953

A Soviet ukaz of 20 January 1953 awarding the cardiologist Lydia Timashuk the Order of Lenin for "unmasking doctors-killers." It was revoked after Stalin's death later that year.
In his later years, Stalin was in poor health.[628] He took increasingly long holidays; in 1950 and again in 1951 he spent almost five months vacationing at his Abkhazian dacha.[629] Stalin nevertheless mistrusted his doctors; in January 1952 he had one imprisoned after they suggested that he should retire to improve his health.[628] In September 1952, several Kremlin doctors were arrested for allegedly plotting to kill senior politicians in what came to be known as the Doctors' Plot; the majority of the accused were Jewish.[630] He instructed the arrested doctors to be tortured to ensure confession.[631] In November, the Slánský trial took place in Czechoslovakia as 13 senior Communist Party figures, 11 of them Jewish, were accused and convicted of being part of a vast Zionist-American conspiracy to subvert Eastern Bloc governments.[632] That same month, a much publicised trial of accused Jewish industrial wreckers took place in Ukraine.[633] In 1951, he initiated the Mingrelian affair, a purge of the Georgian branch of the Communist Party which resulted in over 11,000 deportations.[634]

From 1946 until his death, Stalin only gave three public speeches, two of which lasted only a few minutes.[635] The amount of written material that he produced also declined.[635] In 1950, Stalin issued the article "Marxism and Problems of Linguistics", which reflected his interest in questions of Russian nationhood.[636] In 1952, Stalin's last book, Economic Problems of Socialism in the USSR, was published. It sought to provide a guide to leading the country after his death.[637] In October 1952, Stalin gave an hour and a half speech at the Central Committee plenum.[638] There, he emphasised what he regarded as leadership qualities necessary in the future and highlighted the weaknesses of various potential successors, particularly Molotov and Mikoyan.[639] In 1952, he also eliminated the Politburo and replaced it with a larger version which he called the Presidium.[640]

Death, funeral and aftermath: 1953
Main article: Death and state funeral of Joseph Stalin
On 1 March 1953, Stalin's staff found him semi-conscious on the bedroom floor of his Volynskoe dacha.[641] He had suffered a cerebral hemorrhage.[642] He was moved onto a couch and remained there for three days.[643] He was hand-fed using a spoon, given various medicines and injections, and leeches were applied to him.[642] Svetlana and Vasily were called to the dacha on 2 March; the latter was drunk and angrily shouted at the doctors, resulting in him being sent home.[644] Stalin died on 5 March 1953.[645] According to Svetlana, it had been "a difficult and terrible death".[646] An autopsy revealed that he had died of a cerebral hemorrhage and that he also suffered from severe damage to his cerebral arteries due to atherosclerosis.[647] It is possible that Stalin was murdered.[648] Beria has been suspected of murder, although no firm evidence has ever appeared.[642]

Stalin's death was announced on 6 March.[649] The body was embalmed,[650] and then placed on display in Moscow's House of Unions for three days.[651] Crowds were such that a crush killed around 100 people.[652] The funeral involved the body being laid to rest in Lenin's Mausoleum in Red Square on 9 March; hundreds of thousands attended.[653] That month featured a surge in arrests for "anti-Soviet agitation" as those celebrating Stalin's death came to police attention.[654] The Chinese government instituted a period of official mourning for Stalin's death.[655]

Stalin left no anointed successor nor a framework within which a transfer of power could take place.[656] The Central Committee met on the day of his death, with Malenkov, Beria, and Khrushchev emerging as the party's key figures.[657] The system of collective leadership was restored, and measures introduced to prevent any one member attaining autocratic domination again.[658] The collective leadership included the following eight senior members of the Presidium of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union listed according to the order of precedence presented formally on 5 March 1953: Georgy Malenkov, Lavrentiy Beria, Vyacheslav Molotov, Kliment Voroshilov, Nikita Khrushchev, Nikolai Bulganin, Lazar Kaganovich and Anastas Mikoyan.[659] Reforms to the Soviet system were immediately implemented.[660] Economic reform scaled back the mass construction projects, placed a new emphasis on house building, and eased the levels of taxation on the peasantry to stimulate production.[661] The new leaders sought rapprochement with Yugoslavia and a less hostile relationship with the U.S.,[662] pursuing a negotiated end to the Korean War in July 1953.[663] The doctors who had been imprisoned were released and the anti-Semitic purges ceased.[664] A mass amnesty for those imprisoned for non-political crimes was issued, halving the country's inmate population, while the state security and Gulag systems were reformed, with torture being banned in April 1953.[661]

Political ideology
Further information: Stalinism

A mourning parade in honor of Stalin in Dresden, East Germany
Stalin claimed to have embraced Marxism at the age of fifteen,[665] and it served as the guiding philosophy throughout his adult life;[666] according to Kotkin, Stalin held "zealous Marxist convictions",[667] while Montefiore suggested that Marxism held a "quasi-religious" value for Stalin.[668] Although he never became a Georgian nationalist,[669] during his early life elements from Georgian nationalist thought blended with Marxism in his outlook.[670] The historian Alfred J. Rieber noted that he had been raised in "a society where rebellion was deeply rooted in folklore and popular rituals".[669] Stalin believed in the need to adapt Marxism to changing circumstances; in 1917, he declared that "there is dogmatic Marxism and there is creative Marxism. I stand on the ground of the latter".[671] Volkogonov believed that Stalin's Marxism was shaped by his "dogmatic turn of mind", suggesting that this had been instilled in the Soviet leader during his education in religious institutions.[672] According to scholar Robert Service, Stalin's "few innovations in ideology were crude, dubious developments of Marxism".[666] Some of these derived from political expediency rather than any sincere intellectual commitment;[666] Stalin would often turn to ideology post hoc to justify his decisions.[673] Stalin referred to himself as a praktik, meaning that he was more of a practical revolutionary than a theoretician.[674]

As a Marxist and an extreme anti-capitalist, Stalin believed in an inevitable "class war" between the world's proletariat and bourgeoise.[675] He believed that the working classes would prove successful in this struggle and would establish a dictatorship of the proletariat,[676] regarding the Soviet Union as an example of such a state.[677] He also believed that this proletarian state would need to introduce repressive measures against foreign and domestic "enemies" to ensure the full crushing of the propertied classes,[678] and thus the class war would intensify with the advance of socialism.[679] As a propaganda tool, the shaming of "enemies" explained all inadequate economic and political outcomes, the hardships endured by the populace, and military failures.[680] The new state would then be able to ensure that all citizens had access to work, food, shelter, healthcare, and education, with the wastefulness of capitalism eliminated by a new, standardised economic system.[681] According to Sandle, Stalin was "committed to the creation of a society that was industrialised, collectivised, centrally planned and technologically advanced."[682]

Stalin adhered to the Leninist variant of Marxism.[683] In his book, Foundations of Leninism, he stated that "Leninism is the Marxism of the epoch of imperialism and of the proletarian revolution".[684] He claimed to be a loyal Leninist,[685] although was—according to Service—"not a blindly obedient Leninist".[681] Stalin respected Lenin, but not uncritically,[686] and spoke out when he believed that Lenin was wrong.[681] During the period of his revolutionary activity, Stalin regarded some of Lenin's views and actions as being the self-indulgent activities of a spoiled émigré, deeming them counterproductive for those Bolshevik activists based within the Russian Empire itself.[687] After the October Revolution, they continued to have differences. Whereas Lenin believed that all countries across Europe and Asia would readily unite as a single state following proletariat revolution, Stalin argued that national pride would prevent this, and that different socialist states would have to be formed; in his view, a country like Germany would not readily submit to being part of a Russian-dominated federal state.[688] Stalin biographer Oleg Khlevniuk nevertheless believed that the pair developed a "strong bond" over the years,[689] while Kotkin suggested that Stalin's friendship with Lenin was "the single most important relationship in Stalin's life".[690] After Lenin's death, Stalin relied heavily on Lenin's writings—far more so than those of Marx and Engels—to guide him in the affairs of state.[691] Stalin adopted the Leninist view on the need for a revolutionary vanguard who could lead the proletariat rather than being led by them.[676] Leading this vanguard, he believed that the Soviet peoples needed a strong, central figure—akin to a Tsar—whom they could rally around.[692] In his words, "the people need a Tsar, whom they can worship and for whom they can live and work".[693] He read about, and admired, two Tsars in particular: Ivan the Terrible and Peter the Great.[694] In the personality cult constructed around him, he was known as the vozhd, an equivalent to the Italian duce and German fuhrer.[695]


A statue of Stalin in Grūtas Park near Druskininkai, Lithuania; it originally stood in Vilnius, Lithuania
Stalinism was a development of Leninism,[696] and while Stalin avoided using the term "Marxism-Leninism-Stalinism", he allowed others to do so.[697] Following Lenin's death, Stalin contributed to the theoretical debates within the Communist Party, namely by developing the idea of "Socialism in One Country". This concept was intricately linked to factional struggles within the party, particularly against Trotsky.[698] He first developed the idea in December 1924 and elaborated upon in his writings of 1925–26.[699] Stalin's doctrine held that socialism could be completed in Russia but that its final victory there could not be guaranteed because of the threat from capitalist intervention. For this reason, he retained the Leninist view that world revolution was still a necessity to ensure the ultimate victory of socialism.[699] Although retaining the Marxist belief that the state would wither away as socialism transformed into pure communism, he believed that the Soviet state would remain until the final defeat of international capitalism.[700] This concept synthesised Marxist and Leninist ideas with nationalist ideals,[682] and served to discredit Trotsky—who promoted the idea of "permanent revolution"—by presenting the latter as a defeatist with little faith in Russian workers' abilities to construct socialism.[701]

Stalin viewed nations as contingent entities which were formed by capitalism and could merge into others.[702] Ultimately he believed that all nations would merge into a single, global human community,[702] and regarded all nations as inherently equal.[703] In his work, he stated that "the right of secession" should be offered to the ethnic-minorities of the Russian Empire, but that they should not be encouraged to take that option.[704] He was of the view that if they became fully autonomous, then they would end up being controlled by the most reactionary elements of their community; as an example he cited the largely illiterate Tatars, whom he claimed would end up dominated by their mullahs.[704] Stalin argued that the Jews possessed a "national character" but were not a "nation" and were thus unassimilable. He argued that Jewish nationalism, particularly Zionism, was hostile to socialism.[705] According to Khlevniuk, Stalin reconciled Marxism with great-power imperialism and therefore expansion of the empire makes him a worthy to the Russian tsars.[680] Service argued that Stalin's Marxism was imbued with a great deal of Russian nationalism.[666] According to Montefiore, Stalin's embrace of the Russian nation was pragmatic, as the Russians were the core of the population of the USSR; it was not a rejection of his Georgian origins.[706] Stalin's push for Soviet westward expansion into eastern Europe resulted in accusations of Russian imperialism.[707]

Personal life and characteristics
Stalin brutally, artfully, indefatigably built a personal dictatorship within the Bolshevik dictatorship. Then he launched and saw through a bloody socialist remaking of the entire former empire, presided over a victory in the greatest war in human history, and took the Soviet Union to the epicentre of global affairs. More than for any other historical figure, even Gandhi or Churchill, a biography of Stalin... eventually comes to approximate a history of the world.
— Stephen Kotkin[708]
Ethnically Georgian,[709] Stalin grew up speaking the Georgian language,[710] and did not begin learning Russian until the age of eight or nine.[711] He remained proud of his Georgian identity,[712] and throughout his life retained a heavy Georgian accent when speaking Russian.[713] According to Montefiore, despite Stalin's affinity for Russia and Russians, he remained profoundly Georgian in his lifestyle and personality.[714] Stalin's colleagues described him as "Asiatic", and he told a Japanese journalist that "I am not a European man, but an Asian, a Russified Georgian".[715] Service also noted that Stalin "would never be Russian", could not credibly pass as one, and never tried to pretend that he was.[716] Montefiore was of the view that "after 1917, [Stalin] became quadri-national: Georgian by nationality, Russian by loyalty, internationalist by ideology, Soviet by citizenship."[717]

Stalin had a soft voice,[718] and when speaking Russian did so slowly, carefully choosing his phrasing.[709] In private he used coarse language, although avoided doing so in public.[719] Described as a poor orator,[720] according to Volkogonov, Stalin's speaking style was "simple and clear, without flights of fancy, catchy phrases or platform histrionics".[721] He rarely spoke before large audiences, and preferred to express himself in written form.[722] His writing style was similar, being characterised by its simplicity, clarity, and conciseness.[723] Throughout his life, he used various nicknames and pseudonyms, including "Koba", "Soselo", and "Ivanov",[724] adopting "Stalin" in 1912; it was based on the Russian word for "steel" and has often been translated as "Man of Steel".[140]


Lavrenti Beria with Stalin's daughter, Svetlana, on his lap and Stalin seated in the background. Stalin's dacha near Sochi, mid-1930s.
In adulthood, Stalin measured 5 feet 4 inches (1.63 m) tall.[725] To appear taller, he wore stacked shoes, and stood on a small platform during parades.[726] His mustached face was pock-marked from smallpox during childhood; this was airbrushed from published photographs.[727] He was born with a webbed left foot, and his left arm had been permanently injured in childhood which left it shorter than his right and lacking in flexibility,[728] which was probably the result of being hit, at the age of 12, by a horse-drawn carriage.[729]

During his youth, Stalin cultivated a scruffy appearance in rejection of middle-class aesthetic values.[730] He grew his hair long and often wore a beard; for clothing, he often wore a traditional Georgian chokha or a red satin shirt with a grey coat and red fedora.[731] From mid-1918 until his death he favoured military-style clothing, in particular long black boots, light-coloured collarless tunics, and a gun.[732] He was a lifelong smoker, who smoked both a pipe and cigarettes.[733] He had few material demands and lived plainly, with simple and inexpensive clothing and furniture;[734] his interest was in power rather than wealth.[735]

As Soviet leader, Stalin typically awoke around 11 am,[736] with lunch being served between 3 and 5 pm and dinner no earlier than 9 pm;[737] he then worked late into the evening.[738] He often dined with other Politburo members and their families.[739] As leader, he rarely left Moscow unless to go to one of his dachas;[740] he disliked travel,[741] and refused to travel by plane.[742] His choice of favoured holiday house changed over the years,[743] although he holidayed in southern parts of the USSR every year from 1925 to 1936 and again from 1945 to 1951.[744] Along with other senior figures, he had a dacha at Zubalova, 35 km outside Moscow,[745] although ceased using it after Nadya's 1932 suicide.[746] After 1932, he favoured holidays in Abkhazia, being a friend of its leader, Nestor Lakoba.[747] In 1934, his new Kuntsevo Dacha was built; 9 km from the Kremlin, it became his primary residence.[748] In 1935 he began using a new dacha provided for him by Lakoba at Novy Afon;[749] in 1936, he had the Kholodnaya Rechka dacha built on the Abkhazian coast, designed by Miron Merzhanov.[750]

Personality

Chinese Marxists celebrate Stalin's seventieth birthday in 1949
Trotsky and several other Soviet figures promoted the idea that Stalin was a mediocrity.[751] This gained widespread acceptance outside the Soviet Union during his lifetime but was misleading.[752] According to Montefiore, "it is clear from hostile and friendly witnesses alike that Stalin was always exceptional, even from childhood".[752] Stalin had a complex mind,[753] great self-control,[754] and an excellent memory.[755] He was a hard worker,[756] and displayed a keen desire to learn;[757] when in power, he scrutinised many details of Soviet life, from film scripts to architectural plans and military hardware.[758] According to Volkogonov, "Stalin's private life and working life were one and the same"; he did not take days off from political activities.[759]

Stalin could play different roles to different audiences,[760] and was adept at deception, often deceiving others as to his true motives and aims.[761] Several historians have seen it appropriate to follow Lazar Kaganovich's description of there being "several Stalins" as a means of understanding his multi-faceted personality.[762] He was a good organiser,[763] with a strategic mind,[764] and judged others according to their inner strength, practicality, and cleverness.[765] He acknowledged that he could be rude and insulting,[766] although rarely raised his voice in anger;[767] as his health deteriorated in later life he became increasingly unpredictable and bad tempered.[768] Despite his tough-talking attitude, he could be very charming;[769] when relaxed, he cracked jokes and mimicked others.[757] Montefiore suggested that this charm was "the foundation of Stalin's power in the Party".[770]

Stalin was ruthless,[771] temperamentally cruel,[772] and had a propensity for violence high even among the Bolsheviks.[767] He lacked compassion,[773] something Volkogonov suggested might have been accentuated by his many years in prison and exile,[774] although he was capable of acts of kindness to strangers, even amid the Great Terror.[775] He was capable of self-righteous indignation,[776] and was resentful,[777] vindictive,[778] and vengeful, holding onto grievances against others for many years.[779] By the 1920s, he was also suspicious and conspiratorial, prone to believing that people were plotting against him and that there were vast international conspiracies behind acts of dissent.[780] He never attended torture sessions or executions,[781] although Service thought Stalin "derived deep satisfaction" from degrading and humiliating people and keeping even close associates in a state of "unrelieved fear".[707] Montefiore thought Stalin's brutality marked him out as a "natural extremist";[782] Service suggested he had a paranoid or sociopathic personality disorder.[753] Other historians linked his brutality not to any personality trait, but to his unwavering commitment to the survival of the Soviet Union and the international Marxist–Leninist cause.[783]

It is hard for me to reconcile the courtesy and consideration he showed me personally with the ghastly cruelty of his wholesale liquidations. Others, who did not know him personally, see only the tyrant in Stalin. I saw the other side as well – his high intelligence, that fantastic grasp of detail, his shrewdness and his surprising human sensitivity that he was capable of showing, at least in the war years. I found him better informed than Roosevelt, more realistic than Churchill, in some ways the most effective of the war leaders... I must confess that for me Stalin remains the most inscrutable and contradictory character I have known – and leave the final word to the judgment of history.
— U.S. ambassador W. Averell Harriman[784]
Keenly interested in the arts,[785] Stalin admired artistic talent.[786] He protected several Soviet writers, such as Mikhail Bulgakov, even when their work was labelled harmful to his regime.[787] He enjoyed music,[788] owning around 2,700 albums,[789] and frequently attending the Bolshoi Theatre during the 1930s and 1940s.[790] His taste in music and theatre was conservative, favouring classical drama, opera, and ballet over what he dismissed as experimental "formalism".[711] He also favoured classical forms in the visual arts, disliking avant-garde styles like cubism and futurism.[791] He was a voracious reader, with a library of over 20,000 books.[792] Little of this was fiction,[793] although he could cite passages from Alexander Pushkin, Nikolay Nekrasov, and Walt Whitman by heart.[786] He favoured historical studies, keeping up with debates in the study of Russian, Mesopotamian, ancient Roman, and Byzantine history.[635] An autodidact,[794] he claimed to read as many as 500 pages a day,[795] with Montefiore regarding him as an intellectual.[796] Stalin also enjoyed watching films late at night at cinemas installed in the Kremlin and his dachas.[797] He favoured the Western genre;[798] his favourite film was the 1938 picture Volga Volga.[799]

Stalin was a keen and accomplished billiards player,[800] and collected watches.[801] He also enjoyed practical jokes; he for instance would place a tomato on the seat of Politburo members and wait for them to sit on it.[802] When at social events, he encouraged singing,[803] as well as alcohol consumption; he hoped that others would drunkenly reveal their secrets to him.[804] As an infant, Stalin displayed a love of flowers,[805] and later in life he became a keen gardener.[805] His Volynskoe suburb had a 20-hectare (50-acre) park, with Stalin devoting much attention to its agricultural activities.[806]

Stalin publicly condemned anti-Semitism,[807] although was repeatedly accused of it.[808] People who knew him, such as Khrushchev, suggested he long harbored negative sentiments toward Jews,[809] and anti-Semitic trends in his policies were further fueled by Stalin's struggle against Trotsky.[810] After Stalin's death, Khrushchev claimed that Stalin encouraged him to incite anti-Semitism in Ukraine, allegedly telling him that "the good workers at the factory should be given clubs so they can beat the hell out of those Jews."[811] In 1946, Stalin allegedly said privately that "every Jew is a potential spy."[812] Conquest stated that although Stalin had Jewish associates, he promoted anti-Semitism.[813] Service cautioned that there was "no irrefutable evidence" of anti-Semitism in Stalin's published work, although his private statements and public actions were "undeniably reminiscent of crude antagonism towards Jews";[814] he added that throughout Stalin's lifetime, the Georgian "would be the friend, associate or leader of countless individual Jews".[815] According to Beria, Stalin had affairs with several Jewish women.[816]

Relationships and family

Stalin carrying his daughter, Svetlana
Friendship was important to Stalin,[817] and he used it to gain and maintain power.[818] Kotkin observed that Stalin "generally gravitated to people like himself: parvenu intelligentsia of humble background".[819] He gave nicknames to his favourites, for instance referring to Yezhov as "my blackberry".[820] Stalin was sociable and enjoyed a joke.[821] According to Montefiore, Stalin's friendships "meandered between love, admiration, and venomous jealousy".[822] While head of the Soviet Union he remained in contact with many of his old friends in Georgia, sending them letters and gifts of money.[823]

Stalin was attracted to women and there are no reports of any homosexual tendencies;[824] according to Montefiore, in his early life Stalin "rarely seems to have been without a girlfriend".[825] He was sexually promiscuous, although rarely talked about his sex life.[826] Montefiore noted that Stalin's favoured types were "young, malleable teenagers or buxom peasant women",[826] who would be supportive and unchallenging toward him.[827] According to Service, Stalin "regarded women as a resource for sexual gratification and domestic comfort".[828] Stalin married twice and had several offspring.[824]

Stalin married his first wife, Ekaterina Svanidze, in 1906. According to Montefiore, theirs was "a true love match";[829] Volkogonov suggested that she was "probably the one human being he had really loved".[830] When she died Stalin said "This creature softened my heart of stone."[831] They had a son, Yakov, who often frustrated and annoyed Stalin.[832] Yakov had a daughter, Galina, before fighting for the Red Army in the Second World War. He was captured by the German Army and then committed suicide.[833]

Stalin's second wife was Nadezhda Alliluyeva; theirs was not an easy relationship, and they often fought.[834] They had two biological children—a son, Vasily, and a daughter, Svetlana—and adopted another son, Artyom Sergeev, in 1921.[835] During his marriage to Nadezhda, Stalin had affairs with many other women, most of whom were fellow revolutionaries or their wives.[836] Nadezdha suspected that this was the case,[837] and committed suicide in 1932.[838] Stalin regarded Vasily as spoiled and often chastised his behaviour; as Stalin's son, Vasily nevertheless was swiftly promoted through the ranks of the Red Army and allowed a lavish lifestyle.[839] Conversely, Stalin had an affectionate relationship with Svetlana during her childhood,[840] and was also very fond of Artyom.[835] In later life, he disapproved of Svetlana's various suitors and husbands, putting a strain on his relationship with her.[841] After the Second World War he made little time for his children and his family played a decreasingly important role in his life.[842] After Stalin's death, Svetlana changed her surname from Stalin to Allilueva,[662] and defected to the U.S.[843]

After Nadezdha's death, Stalin became increasingly close to his sister-in-law Zhenya Alliluyeva;[844] Montefiore believed that they were probably lovers.[845] There are unproven rumours that from 1934 onward he had a relationship with his housekeeper Valentina Istomina.[846] Stalin had at least two illegitimate children,[847] although he never recognised them as being his.[848] One of them, Konstantin Kuzakov, later taught philosophy at the Leningrad Military Mechanical Institute, but never met his father.[849] The other, Alexander, was the son of Lidia Pereprygia; he was raised as the son of a peasant fisherman and the Soviet authorities made him swear never to reveal that Stalin was his biological father.[850]

Legacy

A poster of Stalin at the 3rd World Festival of Youth and Students in East Berlin, East Germany, 1951
The historian Robert Conquest stated that Stalin, "perhaps[…] determined the course of the twentieth century" more than any other individual.[851] Biographers like Service and Volkogonov have considered him an outstanding and exceptional politician;[852] Montefiore labelled Stalin as "that rare combination: both 'intellectual' and killer", a man who was "the ultimate politician" and "the most elusive and fascinating of the twentieth-century titans".[853] According to historian Kevin McDermott, interpretations of Stalin range from "the sycophantic and adulatory to the vitriolic and condemnatory".[854] For most Westerners and anti-communist Russians, he is viewed overwhelmingly negatively as a mass murderer;[854] for significant numbers of Russians and Georgians, he is regarded as a great statesman and state-builder.[854]

Stalin strengthened and stabilised the Soviet Union;[855] Service suggested that without him the country might have collapsed long before 1991.[855] In under three decades, Stalin transformed the Soviet Union into a major industrial world power,[856] one which could "claim impressive achievements" in terms of urbanisation, military strength, education, and Soviet pride.[857] Under his rule, the average Soviet life expectancy grew due to improved living conditions, nutrition, and medical care;[858] mortality rates also declined.[859] Although millions of Soviet citizens despised him, support for Stalin was nevertheless widespread throughout Soviet society.[857]

Stalin's Soviet Union has been characterised as a totalitarian state,[860] with Stalin its authoritarian leader.[861] Various biographers have described him as a dictator,[862] an autocrat,[863] or accused him of practicing Caesarism.[864] Montefiore argued that while Stalin initially ruled as part of a Communist Party oligarchy, in 1934 the Soviet government transformed from this oligarchy into a personal dictatorship,[865] with Stalin only becoming "absolute dictator" between March and June 1937, when senior military and NKVD figures were eliminated.[866] According to Kotkin, Stalin "built a personal dictatorship within the Bolshevik dictatorship".[708] In both the Soviet Union and elsewhere he came to be portrayed as an "Oriental despot".[867] The biographer Dmitri Volkogonov characterised him as "one of the most powerful figures in human history",[868] while McDermott stated that Stalin had "concentrated unprecedented political authority in his hands",[869] and Service noted that by the late 1930s, Stalin "had come closer to personal despotism than almost any monarch in history".[870]


A contingent from the Communist Party of Great Britain (Marxist–Leninist) carrying a banner of Stalin at a May Day march through London in 2008.
McDermott nevertheless cautioned against "over-simplistic stereotypes"—promoted in the fiction of writers like Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn, Vasily Grossman, and Anatoly Rybakov—that portrayed Stalin as an omnipotent and omnipresent tyrant who controlled every aspect of Soviet life through repression and totalitarianism.[871] Service similarly warned of the portrayal of Stalin as an "unimpeded despot", noting that "powerful though he was, his powers were not limitless", and his rule depended on his willingness to conserve the Soviet structure he had inherited.[872] Kotkin observed that Stalin's ability to remain in power relied on him having a majority in the Politburo at all times.[873] Khlevniuk noted that at various points, particularly when Stalin was old and frail, there were "periodic manifestations" in which the party oligarchy threatened his autocratic control.[768] Stalin denied to foreign visitors that he was a dictator, stating that those who labelled him such did not understand the Soviet governance structure.[874]

A vast literature devoted to Stalin has been produced.[875] During Stalin's lifetime, his approved biographies were largely hagiographic in content.[876] Stalin ensured that these works gave very little attention to his early life, particularly because he did not wish to emphasise his Georgian origins in a state numerically dominated by Russians.[877] Since his death many more biographies have been written,[878] although until the 1980s these relied largely on the same sources of information.[878] Under Mikhail Gorbachev's Soviet administration various previously classified files on Stalin's life were made available to historians,[878] at which point Stalin became "one of the most urgent and vital issues on the public agenda" in the Soviet Union.[879] After the dissolution of the Union in 1991, the rest of the archives were opened to historians, resulting in much new information about Stalin coming to light,[880] and producing a flood of new research.[875]

Leninists remain divided in their views on Stalin; some view him as Lenin's authentic successor, while others believe he betrayed Lenin's ideas by deviating from them.[707] The socio-economic nature of Stalin's Soviet Union has also been much debated, varyingly being labelled a form of state socialism, state capitalism, bureaucratic collectivism, or a totally unique mode of production.[881] Socialist writers like Volkogonov have acknowledged that Stalin's actions damaged "the enormous appeal of socialism generated by the October Revolution".[882]

Death toll and allegations of genocide
Main article: Excess mortality in the Soviet Union under Joseph Stalin
With a high number of excess deaths occurring under his rule, Stalin has been labeled "one of the most notorious figures in history".[855] These deaths occurred as a result of collectivisation, famine, terror campaigns, disease, war and mortality rates in the Gulag. As the majority of excess deaths under Stalin were not direct killings, the exact number of victims of Stalinism is difficult to calculate due to lack of consensus among scholars on which deaths can be attributed to the regime.[883]


Interior of the Gulag Museum in Moscow

Victims of Stalin's Great Terror in the Bykivnia mass graves
Official records reveal 799,455 documented executions in the Soviet Union between 1921 and 1953; 681,692 of these were carried out between 1937 and 1938, the years of the Great Purge.[884] However, according to Michael Ellman, the best modern estimate for the number of repression deaths during the Great Purge is 950,000–1.2 million, which includes executions, deaths in detention, or soon after their release.[885] In addition, while archival data shows that 1,053,829 perished in the Gulag from 1934 to 1953,[886] the current historical consensus is that of the 18 million people who passed through the Gulag system from 1930 to 1953, between 1.5 and 1.7 million died as a result of their incarceration.[887] The historian and archival researcher Stephen G. Wheatcroft and Michael Ellman attribute roughly 3 million deaths to the Stalinist regime, including executions and deaths from criminal negligence.[888][889] Wheatcoft and historian Robert Davies estimate famine deaths at 5.5–6.5 million[890] while scholar Steven Rosefielde gives a number of 8.7 million.[891] The American historian Timothy D. Snyder in 2011 summarised modern data, made after the opening of the Soviet archives in the 1990s, and concludes that Stalin's regime was responsible for 9 million deaths, with 6 million of these being deliberate killings. He notes that the estimate is far lower than the estimates of 20 million or above which were made before access to the archives.[892]

Historians continue to debate whether or not the 1932–33 Ukrainian famine—known in Ukraine as the Holodomor—should be called a genocide.[893] Twenty-six countries officially recognise it under the legal definition of genocide. In 2006, the Ukrainian Parliament declared it to be such,[894] and in 2010 a Ukrainian court posthumously convicted Stalin, Lazar Kaganovich, Stanislav Kosior, and other Soviet leaders of genocide.[895][896] Popular among some Ukrainian nationalists is the idea that Stalin consciously organised the famine to suppress national desires among the Ukrainian people. This interpretation has been rejected by more recent historical studies.[897] These have articulated the view that—while Stalin's policies contributed significantly to the high mortality rate—there is no evidence that Stalin or the Soviet government consciously engineered the famine.[898][899] The idea that this was a targeted attack on the Ukrainians is complicated by the widespread suffering that also affected other Soviet peoples in the famine, including the Russians.[900] Within Ukraine, ethnic Poles and Bulgarians died in similar proportions to ethnic Ukrainians.[901] Despite any lack of clear intent on Stalin's part, the historian Norman Naimark noted that although there may not be sufficient "evidence to convict him in an international court of justice as a genocidaire[...] that does not mean that the event itself cannot be judged as genocide".[902]

In the Soviet Union and its successor states

Interior of the Joseph Stalin Museum in Gori, Georgia
Shortly after his death, the Soviet Union went through a period of de-Stalinization. Malenkov denounced the Stalin personality cult,[903] which was subsequently criticised in Pravda.[904] In 1956, Khrushchev gave his "Secret Speech", titled "On the Cult of Personality and Its Consequences", to a closed session of the Party's 20th Congress. There, Khrushchev denounced Stalin for both his mass repression and his personality cult.[905] He repeated these denunciations at the 22nd Party Congress in October 1962.[906] In October 1961, Stalin's body was removed from the mausoleum and buried in the Kremlin Wall Necropolis next to the Kremlin walls, the location marked only by a simple bust.[907] Stalingrad was renamed Volgograd.[908]

Khrushchev's de-Stalinisation process in Soviet society ended when he was replaced as leader by Leonid Brezhnev in 1964; the latter introduced a level of re-Stalinisation within the Soviet Union.[909] In 1969 and again in 1979, plans were proposed for a full rehabilitation of Stalin's legacy, but on both occasions were defeated by critics within the Soviet and international Marxist–Leninist movement.[910] Gorbachev saw the total denunciation of Stalin as necessary for the regeneration of Soviet society.[911] After the fall of the Soviet Union in 1991, the first President of the new Russian Federation, Boris Yeltsin, continued Gorbachev's denunciation of Stalin but added to it a denunciation of Lenin.[911] His successor, Vladimir Putin, did not seek to rehabilitate Stalin but emphasised the celebration of Soviet achievements under Stalin's leadership rather than the Stalinist repressions;[912] however, in October 2017 Putin opened the Wall of Grief memorial in Moscow, noting that the "terrible past" would neither be "justified by anything" nor "erased from the national memory".[913]


Marxist–Leninist activists from the opposition Communist Party of Russia laying wreaths at Stalin's Moscow grave in 2009
Amid the social and economic turmoil of the post-Soviet period, many Russians viewed Stalin as having overseen an era of order, predictability, and pride.[914] He remains a revered figure among many Russian nationalists, who feel nostalgic about the Soviet victory over Nazi Germany in World War II,[915] and he is regularly invoked approvingly within both Russia's far-left and far-right.[916] In the 2008 Name of Russia television show, Stalin was voted as the third most notable personality in Russian history.[917] Polling by the Levada Center suggest Stalin's popularity has grown since 2015, with 46% of Russians expressing a favourable view of him in 2017 and 51% in 2019.[918][919] At the same time, there was a growth in pro-Stalinist literature in Russia, much relying upon the misrepresentation or fabrication of source material.[920] In this literature, Stalin's repressions are regarded either as a necessary measure to defeat "enemies of the people" or the result of lower-level officials acting without Stalin's knowledge.[920]

The only part of the former Soviet Union where admiration for Stalin has remained consistently widespread is Georgia.[921] Many Georgians resent criticism of Stalin, the most famous figure from their nation's modern history;[915] a 2013 survey by Tbilisi University found 45% of Georgians expressing "a positive attitude" to him.[922] Some positive sentiment can also be found elsewhere in the former Soviet Union. A 2012 survey commissioned by the Carnegie Endowment found 38% of Armenians concurring that their county "will always have need of a leader like Stalin".[923][924] In early 2010 a new monument to Stalin was erected in Zaporizhia, Ukraine;[896] in December unknown persons cut off its head and in 2011 it was destroyed in an explosion.[925] In a 2016 Kiev International Institute of Sociology poll, 38% of respondents had a negative attitude to Stalin, 26% a neutral one and 17% a positive (19% refused to answer).[926]

Vladimir Ilyich Lenin (tiếng Nga: Влади́мир Ильи́ч Ле́нин, phiên âm tiếng Việt: Vla-đi-mia I-lích Lê-nin), tên khai sinh là Vladimir Ilyich Ulyanov (tiếng Nga: Влади́мир Ильи́ч Улья́нов), còn thường được gọi với tên V. I. Lenin hay N. Lenin, có các bí danh: V.Ilin, K.Tulin, Karpov...; sinh ngày 22 tháng 4 năm 1870, mất ngày 21 tháng 1 năm 1924; là một lãnh tụ của phong trào cách mạng vô sản Nga, là người phát triển học thuyết của Karl Marx và Friedrich Engels.

Ông sinh tại làng Gorki, Simbirsk, nay là Ulyanovsk. Tên họ thật là Vladimir Ilyich Ulyanov. Lenin là người tổ chức Đảng Cộng sản Liên Xô và lãnh đạo Cách mạng Tháng Mười thành lập nước Nga Xô Viết.

Những người cộng sản gọi ông là nhà tư tưởng vĩ đại, nhà lý luận chính trị kiệt xuất, lãnh tụ lỗi lạc của giai cấp công nhân và nhân dân lao động toàn thế giới. Ông được tạp chí Time đánh giá là một trong 100 nhân vật có ảnh hưởng nhất đến lịch sử thế giới[1].

Ông mất vào tháng 1 năm 1924, thi hài được lưu giữ trong lăng Lenin trên Quảng trường Đỏ, Moskva cho tới nay. Tại nước Nga hiện nay, tên của ông được đặt cho 1 tỉnh của Nga (tỉnh Leningrad, nằm sát cố đô Sankt Peterburg, nơi Lenin lãnh đạo thành công Cách mạng Tháng Mười)[2], thành phố quê hương của Lenin thì được đặt tên là Ulyanovsk để tưởng nhớ ông (Lenin có họ là Ulyanov).
